  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13355593551750468227"><em>They open a door and enter a world</em> <br /><br />NARNIA...the land beyond the wardrobe, the secret country known only to Peter, Susan, Edmund, and Lucy...the place where the adventure begins.<br /><br />Lucy is the first to find the secret of the wardrobe in the professor's mysterious old house. At first, no one believes her when she tells of her adventures in the land of Narnia. But soon Edmund and then Peter and Susan discover the Magic and meet Aslan, the Great Lion, for themselves. In the blink of an eye, their lives are changed forever.</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_7806720').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_7806720').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_7806720').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script></div>

<div class="communityQuestionAndAnswer">
<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/125878-should-i-read-the-lion-the-witch-and-the">Should I read The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe or The Magician's Nephew first?</a>
</div>
<div class="communityQAFooter questionFooter">
<ul class="questionFooterActions">
<li class="footerItem like">
<span id="like_container_community_question_125878" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/125878?resource_type=CommunityQuestion" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_community_question_125878"><span class="likesCount">7 likes</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F65645.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" class="jsLike like_it" rel="nofollow">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp">
<a href="/questions/125878-should-i-read-the-lion-the-witch-and-the">one year ago</a>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionPermalink">
<a href="/questions/125878-should-i-read-the-lion-the-witch-and-the">See all 12 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/8510614-peter-yoder" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1441288834i/8510614._UX100_CR0,12,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/8510614-peter-yoder">Peter Yoder</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">LION FIRST! As someone who has read The Chronicles of Narnia at least eleven times, I know the books inside and out and can state categorically that<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">LION FIRST! As someone who has read The Chronicles of Narnia at least eleven times, I know the books inside and out and can state categorically that reading the books in their original published order is the best way to experience them. Here are the reasons:<br /><br />1. Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe through Silver Chair follow the childhoods of the Pevensies and company, and thematically flow best when not interrupted by Horse and His Boy. They also reveal the world at the best pace for new readers.<br />2. There is a powerful nostalgia factor that you get when reading the Horse and His Boy that is diminished if you read it right after Lion, Witch, and Wardrobe. Magician and Last Battle also play off that nostalgia.<br />3. The Magician's Nephew has allusions to another book that would not make sense if you had read Magician first. It is also experienced much better when you already have a picture of Narnia from the other books. You kind of spoil the surprises if you read it first.<br /><br />If C.S. Lewis' was reorganizing the books to be in chronological order, it was an effort that never made its way into the books. I was flabbergasted when publishers started printing editions differently. Reordering the books in my mind is like recutting Memento. Will it still tell a story? yes. Will the story be as good? no. At least not without serious rework.<br /><br />The published order is:<br />1. The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe<br />2. Prince Caspian<br />3. Voyage of the Dawn Treader<br />4. The Silver Chair<br />5. The Horse and His Boy<br />6. The Magician's Nephew<br />7. The Last Battle<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Depends on how fast you read. I found The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe in the city library as an elementary student and fell in love with it. Not<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Depends on how fast you read. I found The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe in the city library as an elementary student and fell in love with it. Not until I was in my late 20s did I find out that there were more in a series! I found them, bought the paperback series and enjoyed them as a treasure found. I loved Harry Potter series and own it too. I enjoy reading teen and young adult fiction. I enjoy fantasy, sci fi and time travel books. I did not have a clue about the religious overtones. BTW I"m 70years young.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1447783261">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1447783261" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="left imgcol" title="Patrick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" alt="Patrick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1447783261?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="user" title="Patrick">Patrick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-read-growing-up" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-read-growing-up</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-would-blurb</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=best-books-for-kids" class="actionLinkLite">best-books-for-kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1447783261">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4855050961922245097">This is the first book where I chronicled my thoughts as I read through it with my son. I don't know how easy it is for y'all to access the record of those here on Goodreads, but if you're looking for a detailed account of my thoughts on the book, you can look there.<br /><br />I'll say this. I've read a lot of books to my little boy these last couple years, and I can honestly say that This book is among the best. Good, tight writing, good description. Good action. Also there's not a lot of dead space or tr</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4855050961922245097">This is the first book where I chronicled my thoughts as I read through it with my son. I don't know how easy it is for y'all to access the record of those here on Goodreads, but if you're looking for a detailed account of my thoughts on the book, you can look there.<br /><br />I'll say this. I've read a lot of books to my little boy these last couple years, and I can honestly say that This book is among the best. Good, tight writing, good description. Good action. Also there's not a lot of dead space or trashy empty dialogue that just seems to be there to take up space. (That's become a particular peeve lately. And when you're reading a book aloud, it becomes really obvious.) <br /><br />The British slang will be a stumbling block to some. But it's not too bad. And there were a few slight pieces of sexism that I ignored, skipped over, or re-worded on the fly. But honestly, this book was written 60 years ago, and you need to cut it a little slack because of that. And in my opinion, it only needs a little slack. Truth be told, I've read books written this year that have ten times the sexism this one does. <br /><br />Also, I'd like to make it clear that this is the FIRST book of the Narnia Chronicles. This is where you start the series. I'm sorry if you read them in the wrong order, but if you did, it's better than you admit it now, come to grips, and move on with your life knowing the truth.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4855050961922245097">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_163172902">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163172902" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/163172902?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland" class="actionLinkLite">uk-and-ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer163172902">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9195400625363003557">My greatest disappointment in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4000436">'The Screwtape Letters'</a> was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The closest he got to defining goodness was that you could tell the good people from the vague aura of light that surrounded them--and which even shone in their cat. In this book, the cat is much bigger.<br /><br />Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9195400625363003557">My greatest disappointment in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4000436">'The Screwtape Letters'</a> was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The closest he got to defining goodness was that you could tell the good people from the vague aura of light that surrounded them--and which even shone in their cat. In this book, the cat is much bigger.<br /><br />Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him great or impressive. Sure, he helps the kids, but all that makes him is a plot facilitator. He also has his big Jesus moment, but that has the same problem as the original: if he already knows that there will be no lasting negative outcome, how much of a sacrifice is it, really?<br /><br />But then, Aslan isn't based on the original fig-cursing, church-rejecting, rebel Jesus, but the whitewashed version. Like Mickey Mouse, Jesus started out as an oddball troublemaker with his fair share of personality, but becoming the smiling face of a multinational organization bent on world domination takes a lot out of a mascot, whether your magic castle is in California or Rome.<br /><br />Such a visible figure must become universally appealing, universally friendly and loving, lest some subset of followers feel left out. And it's this 'Buddy Christ' tradition from which Aslan springs. Devoid of insight, wisdom, or charm, Aslan is just here to do all the things that our protagonists can't do.<br /><br />This also beggars the question: why didn't Aslan just take care of all this stuff long before the kids arrived? Why did all the animals and fairies and giants have to suffer the pain of an endless winter? We're never given any good reason Aslan had to wait for the kids--since in the end, he does it all on his own, anyways. Sure, Lewis mentions something vague about a prophecy, but in fantasy, prophecy is always a bandaid authors stick over their plot holes: <em>'Uh, the shlubby nobody is a hero because the prophecy says he is--he defeats the ultimate evil because the prophecy says he can'</em>. <br /><br />The only thing the kids do is help run the battle, but this is only necessary because Aslan is absent, and he's only absent because the kids screwed up, meaning the entire thing would have gone off without a hitch if they had never showed up in the first place.<br /><br />In that regard, I have to say Lewis did an excellent job boiling down Christianity into a fable, and leaving the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QRTS_yM89gE">problem of evil</a> completely intact. Some readers suggest that Aslan lets the queen take over to teach the kids a lesson, but is it really worthwhile to let all the inhabitants of a kingdom suffer a century of misery just to teach a few kids about the true meaning of friendship?<br /><br />The villain is just as poorly-constructed, and seems less concerned with defeating her enemies than with being pointlessly capricious. She manages to trick one of the children, but instead of taking advantage of this fact, she immediately makes it clear that she tricked him. I mean, how did someone that incompetent take over in the first place?<br /><br />Selectively stupid characters are silly and convenient, especially as villains, because this completely undermines their role as foil. It is impressive when characters overcome challenges, but not when challenges simply crumble before them. The children are lucky the Queen was more of a <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.pitt.edu/%7Edash/fart.html#devil">fart-stealing Old Nick</a> than a Miltonian Satan, otherwise they never would have stood a chance.<br /><br />It is interesting to look at how many Christian authors have tried to reconcile their faith with complex fairy mythologies; not that Christianity doesn't have its own <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_of_tobit">magical fairy tales</a>, but these other traditions are not exactly compatible. Dante has Virgil lead him through hell, the Buddha was <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barlaam_and_Josaphat">made into a saint</a>, holidays were given new meanings (even if they often kept <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%92ostre">old symbols and names</a>), and magical monsters were also given a place in the new faith.<br /><br />In the Middle Ages, monks compiled <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beastiary">'Bestiaries'</a>, which described the roles of dragons, unicorns, and real animals in Christian synbolism; there were even century-spanning debates about <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cynocephaly#Late_Antiquity">whether dog-headed men were descended from Adam</a>. These books were rarely accurate, but allowed Christian theology to adopt many stories and superstitions from earlier periods; for instance, the connection between unicorns and virginity or the belief that pelicans fed their own blood to their young, in imitation of <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transubstantiation">communion</a>.<br /><br />So Lewis' attempt to take myth and adapt it to a Christian cosmology is hardly new--there is a long and storied tradition explored throughout the Chivalric period and recognizable today in books like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43545.The_Once_and_Future_King">The Once and Future King</a>, but Lewis doesn't do a very good job of reconciling these disparate mythologies.<br /><br />Like most Protestants, Lewis' religion was a modern one--not magical and mystical, but reasonable and utilitarian. He did not draw on the elaborate, convoluted apocrypha of hallucinatory monsters and miracles that mystics obsess over, instead, he made a small, sane, reasonable magical world--which rather defeats the point. It is unfortunate that many of today's readers think of Lewis' writings as defining English fairy tales, since his late additions to the genre are not original, nor are they particularly well-executed examples.<br /><br />Many authors have come to the genre with much more imagination, a deeper sense of wonder, and a more far-reaching exploration of magic. We have examples from <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/571197.Puck_of_Pook_s_Hill">Kipling</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24213.Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland_Through_the_Looking_Glass">Lewis Carroll</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14686">Dunsany</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13624.The_Worm_Ouroboros">Eddison</a>, <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/189746">Morris</a>, and even modern updates by <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust">Gaiman</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201">Clarke</a>. Lewis, like Tolkien, may be a well-known example, but both are rather short-sighted, and neither one achieves as much as the many talented authors who came before.<br /><br />I'm not saying Lewis is bad, merely that he is unremarkable, and is hardly preeminent in fantasy, or even in children's fantasy. However, I do think his fundamental message is a bad one, even if he didn't realize he was creating it. <br /><br />In all his worlds, all his stories, he takes the sorts of people he dislikes, defines them as 'evil', then sets himself apart from them. There is no attempt to comprehend or to come to mutual understanding. I cannot respect a book which encourages people to vilify what they don't understand and to call isolation righteous. If any worldview deserves the epithet of 'evil', it is the sort of willful, prideful, self-indulgent ignorance Lewis displays.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html">My List of Suggested Fantasy Books</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9195400625363003557">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/1008237-manuel" class="left imgcol" title="Manuel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216930773p2/1008237.jpg" alt="Manuel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/21724571?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 31, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1008237-manuel" class="user" title="Manuel">Manuel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21724571">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17451617160897445526">I loved this book.<br />It was first read to me in 4th grade. We would all come in from lunch and our teacher would read to us for about 30 minutes before we would start class.<br /> I remember this book because it wasnt read to us by Mrs Graham, but instead it would be read by Mr Goodwin, her long-haired, bearded, Birkenstock wearing teacher's aid. <br /> Over the next few weeks we were enthralled by this story, we couldnt wait for lunch period to be over so we could hear what was happening in this magic kingdom</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17451617160897445526">I loved this book.<br />It was first read to me in 4th grade. We would all come in from lunch and our teacher would read to us for about 30 minutes before we would start class.<br /> I remember this book because it wasnt read to us by Mrs Graham, but instead it would be read by Mr Goodwin, her long-haired, bearded, Birkenstock wearing teacher's aid. <br /> Over the next few weeks we were enthralled by this story, we couldnt wait for lunch period to be over so we could hear what was happening in this magic kingdom, called Narnia.<br /> From the begining we all identified with Lucy and her siblings. How was it possible that an English girl could transport herself to another place, simply by hiding in a wardrobe? And once through the wardrobe, there was this wonderful and friendly creature called a faun, Mr Tumnus. All this in only the first chapter.<br /> As the chapters progressed we got to know more about the siblings and the other creatures who inhabit Narnia.<br /> Some people critisize C.S Lewis for using too much Christian symbolism, but I was in 4th grade and to me this was the most wonderful and exciting book ever written for children. <br /><br /> When Mr Goodwin finished the book. I instantly went to the library so I could read it myself. I was very proud this was the first book I read "without pictures". To my joy, I discovered there were other books about Narnia and I eventually read all of them too. Evenutually I discovered other wonderful places in other books and I continue to look for them today.<br /> I will always be grateful to Mr Goodwin, he started off by telling me about Narnia, but in the end, he introduced me to so much more through my on going love of books.<br /> Thank you Mr Goodwin, for everything.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17451617160897445526">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer208664579">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15712725601434595581"><br /><strong>A Defence of C.S. Lewis...or a brief attempt at such</strong><br /><br />Some thoughts recently crossed my mind <s>no doubt on their way down-town to relax</s> in regards to arguments one could offer as a defence of the Christian side of this novel. The main arguments against this novel as a 'Christian allegory' that I have heard are: 1)Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure 2)That C.S. Lewis 'preaches' a black and white morality. So I'm going to roughly address them from my perspective and hope it encourages some discussion.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15712725601434595581"><br /><strong>A Defence of C.S. Lewis...or a brief attempt at such</strong><br /><br />Some thoughts recently crossed my mind <s>no doubt on their way down-town to relax</s> in regards to arguments one could offer as a defence of the Christian side of this novel. The main arguments against this novel as a 'Christian allegory' that I have heard are: 1)Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure 2)That C.S. Lewis 'preaches' a black and white morality. So I'm going to roughly address them from my perspective and hope it encourages some discussion.<br /><br />1) I will agree that Aslan is not a strong Christ-figure. Firstly for Aslan to really represent Christ he would have to be true to the gospel story. In other words he would have to be god made into man come to die for all mankind. However as he only dies for the one traitor again it's not sticking true to the Biblical gospel that all have sinned and that Christ was needed as a sacrifice for that sin. If you take things too literally here, C.S. Lewis' novel doesn't make much that much sense theologically as a result. I'll explain where I am/was going with that in a moment.<br /><br />2) I debate that C.S. Lewis preaches in his novel. Occasionally he can be a touch patronising but compared to many authors he rarely slips into such condescension. As for his morality I think you must understand it from the perspective of Christianity. Christianity is about black and white morality essentially: good versus evil, light vs. dark and truth vs. lies etc. It is also very grey in that Christianity is about life and the fact that no one is perfect, that everyone fits into that moral grey area. Of course I explain roughly and inadequately.<br /><br />Ultimately I see that there is room to argue that C.S. Lewis does a poor job of writing an allegorical novel. However I see it as a very subtle novel that unlike others (for instance The Alchemist) does not build its story around expressing an ideology but rather incorporates an ideology into its storytelling. I think that if one wants to criticise this novel it should be for not properly showing the gospel rather than for 'preaching'. I know that I and many others enjoyed the story first before seeing the connection between it and the Biblical tales. I enjoyed it even more afterwards so, then again I could be a tad biased.<br /><br /><strong>Original Review</strong><br /><br />To begin I must note that I grant this such a high rating due to the impact it had on my life. It to me is one novel that were I to pick the one novel that forged a love of books for me it would be The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe. Why? Because I can remember back about twelve years ago when I was homeschooled by my mother as a five year old. We wandered down during winter into the warm back room and she read the first Narnia book to us. The image of a red faun carrying parcels as he passed a growing lamppost would stick with me from that moment (as it stuck with C.S. Lewis). As I learned to read the Narnia books were the first novels I sunk my growing reading teeth into. And to this day I have read and re read the novels back to front (and maybe front to back).<br /><br />The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe is a novel written for both children and adults. It contains highly allegorical elements as C.S.Lewis was a well-known apologetics writer. However he wrote that he did not write his novel as a pure allegory but as a story. And that is what The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe is, a story to be enjoyed by everyone. And although written in simple language the reader can quickly, concisely and easily imagine the world without the clumsy constraints of overused words. I personally cannot imagine a world without these novels.<br /><br />Additional thoughts:<br /><br />1. Just a question at last. And one with a highly philosophical twist to it. Why is it that people so readily condemn those books which are considered as moral tales? You'd think we could do with more morality in such a twisted and confused world regardless of accepting the belief systems. <br /><br />2. I have heard many people describe the entire series as silly and far too preachy. I do not see it that way at all. Trust me if C.S.Lewis wanted to be preachy he would have written a lot more philosophy and less story. Yes I can see how some would call this silly but then I argue that they are missing the point. It's a fairytale type fantasy intended mainly for children (and for those children again as adults or for their parents perhaps). But I argue that as Lewis only wrote this story based on the story of the crucifixion in many ways that it was not intended as a preachy book. My question is that why is it that if I were to base a story along what some call the 'Christian myth' it is claimed as preaching while as if I were to base it on any other mythology or story it would be deemed as merely copying the themes of another mythology? Is this yet another example of doublethink?*<br /><br />*See <a title="1984 by George Orwell" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984">1984</a> (yes go and read it - you'll get what I mean)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15712725601434595581">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10018930404229039294"><em>Su wrote: ""aslan" means " lion" in Turkish"</em><br /><br />True and it was an intentional choice by C.S. Lewis to do things that way well before J.K. Rowling chose t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10018930404229039294"><em>Su wrote: ""aslan" means " lion" in Turkish"</em><br /><br />True and it was an intentional choice by C.S. Lewis to do things that way well before J.K. Rowling chose to use the name Voldemort for her villain (taken from the French for death) and at the same time that J.R.R. Tolkien was picking names for hobbits based on burrowing and their personalities. Both men were language scholars and it shows in their Narnia and Middle Earth works respectively.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer20522082">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4061237641560124609">I just re-read this book and got so much more out of it than the first time. The symbolism &amp; parallels to basic Christianity stuck out. <br />*turkish delight is our human nature, prone to addiction, selfishness and wrongdoing<br />*Peter said about Edmund, "We should go after him. After all he is our brother." Even though he had just betrayed them and was causing grief they didn't mistreat or disown him. <br />*The very mention of Aslan's name caused certain positive feelings to come over them all they didn'</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4061237641560124609">I just re-read this book and got so much more out of it than the first time. The symbolism &amp; parallels to basic Christianity stuck out. <br />*turkish delight is our human nature, prone to addiction, selfishness and wrongdoing<br />*Peter said about Edmund, "We should go after him. After all he is our brother." Even though he had just betrayed them and was causing grief they didn't mistreat or disown him. <br />*The very mention of Aslan's name caused certain positive feelings to come over them all they didn't know why. But it made Edmund feel guilty.<br />*After Ed was returned and his siblings saw him for the first time Aslan said, "Here is your brother and there's no need to talk about what's in the past." They forgave their brother. Aslan neither excused him nor condemned him.<br />*They all knew better than to go into a wardrobe &amp; shut the door as the book mentions a whole bunch of times. We regularly do things when we know better.<br />*The professor makes them think and questions their disbelief in Lucy's story. This is something the movie totally leaves out. "Who would you usually believe, Lucy or Edmund?" etc. Edmund shows the worst side of human nature, to betray &amp; let others down.<br />*I love that Father Christmas comes giving gifts that represent the gifts &amp; talents we each have to help others with and to overcome evil with.<br />There's more but I have to go! Loved the book. And the movie.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/285654370?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 04, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1" class="user" title="Shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1551102.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780006716631" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=travel-books" class="actionLinkLite">travel-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=fantasy-fantastic" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-fantastic</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer285654370">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10803803947049884587">The Role (bibli)call:<br /><br />The big cuddly cat = Jesus. Strange that a lion should be chosen to represent the big man when Lions are notoriously aggressive, solitary carnivores who are more likely to eat any potential apostles than than teach or lead them.<br /><br />The white witch = Satan or Eve the temptress depending on which side of the tree of knowledge you're most likely to be barking up. Famed for a monochrome wardrobe in the A/W line only. Like Alan Rickman in Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves, she has cancel</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10803803947049884587">The Role (bibli)call:<br /><br />The big cuddly cat = Jesus. Strange that a lion should be chosen to represent the big man when Lions are notoriously aggressive, solitary carnivores who are more likely to eat any potential apostles than than teach or lead them.<br /><br />The white witch = Satan or Eve the temptress depending on which side of the tree of knowledge you're most likely to be barking up. Famed for a monochrome wardrobe in the A/W line only. Like Alan Rickman in Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves, she has cancelled Christmas.<br /><br />Edmund = Judas Iscariot. Judas has been proven to be a more astute bargain maker and walks off with 30 pieces of silver for his denials. Edmund gets a box of sweets. <br /><br />Lucy, Peter, Susan = apostles, knights and other positive biblical forces. An unusual scenario given the general hoo-hah about whether or not any of apostles were female (see last supper male/female image debate).<br /><br />Mr Tumnus the faun = an aberration. With his goat like legs and general caprine features you might be forgiven for imagining that he might be an agent of Satan, or Pan or some other pagan deity. Nope. He's on the side of good and not evil and that there throws the nice set of biblical allusions into chaos.<br /><br />Beavers, birds, satyrs, fauns and other ancillary creatures = collateral damage.<br /><br />Plot summary: Icing sugar, picture perfect winter wonderland accessible through the rear of roomy wardrobe handily equipped with high-end (but non PETA approved) all weather garb. Ruled in supremely effective manner by single minded, highly organised, independent woman until arrival of children and large pet. Maybe this book is actually a metaphor for home life in the modern age.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10803803947049884587">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_929524361">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/929524361" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/22313662-laz" class="left imgcol" title="Laz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445857941p2/22313662.jpg" alt="Laz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/929524361?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 27, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/22313662-laz" class="user" title="Laz">Laz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/822603.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780007115617" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone, really</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=magic" class="actionLinkLite">magic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=high-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer929524361">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14202508898697289093">Well, can you blame me for loving this? I certainly hope not. It's Christmas and I feel like a little kid and I was craving something to make me feel like I am one, indeed, and this book travelled me to a wondrous world full of heroes and of course a villain. The ride was awesome and I found the characters warm and fuzzy despite the eternal cold that had been placed upon Narnia. Now, they're all free of the curse of the White Witch thanks to Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy. The kings and queens of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14202508898697289093">Well, can you blame me for loving this? I certainly hope not. It's Christmas and I feel like a little kid and I was craving something to make me feel like I am one, indeed, and this book travelled me to a wondrous world full of heroes and of course a villain. The ride was awesome and I found the characters warm and fuzzy despite the eternal cold that had been placed upon Narnia. Now, they're all free of the curse of the White Witch thanks to Peter, Susan, Edmund and Lucy. The kings and queens of Narnia.<br /><br /><strong>
  <blockquote>“Once a king or queen of Narnia, always a king or queen of Narnia.”</blockquote>
</strong><br /><br />This is remarkable. It's amazing how short it is, I only read it in one and a half hour, I simply devoured it. It's short but so detailed and concentrated. It's like a fairytale. I'll certainly be reading this to my kids in the future. <br /><br /><strong>
  <blockquote>“I wrote this story for you, but when I began it I had not realized that girls grow quicker than books. As a result you are already too old for fairy tales, and by the time it is printed and bound you will be older still. But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again. You can then take it down from some upper shelf, dust it, and tell me what you think of it. I shall probably be too deaf to hear, and too old to understand a word you say, but I shall still be your <br /><br />affectionate Godfather, C. S. Lewis.”</blockquote>
</strong><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_350777668">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350777668" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5486168-dyuti" class="left imgcol" title="Dyuti"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351864861p2/5486168.jpg" alt="Dyuti" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/350777668?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5486168-dyuti" class="user" title="Dyuti">Dyuti</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">All fantasy lovers</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5486168-dyuti?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5486168-dyuti?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer350777668">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17684640087629531623"><strong>What an amazingly delightful book! I regret not reading it earlier</strong><br /><br /><em> ** Before I begin, let me clarify that as I am not a Christian, I had no idea that this book was written by keeping the image of Jesus (as Aslan) in mind. I just came across this revelation on Goodreads, and it just added another layer to the story! The review below is written only by treating it as a fun-filled and action packed novel!</em><br /><br /><img width="400" height="350" class="escapedImg" alt="narnia" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380381163r/738356._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />I had seen the movie based on the book a couple of years ago, and had really loved it! On readi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17684640087629531623"><strong>What an amazingly delightful book! I regret not reading it earlier</strong><br /><br /><em> ** Before I begin, let me clarify that as I am not a Christian, I had no idea that this book was written by keeping the image of Jesus (as Aslan) in mind. I just came across this revelation on Goodreads, and it just added another layer to the story! The review below is written only by treating it as a fun-filled and action packed novel!</em><br /><br /><img width="400" height="350" class="escapedImg" alt="narnia" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380381163r/738356._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />I had seen the movie based on the book a couple of years ago, and had really loved it! On reading the book, I realized it's just an extention of the movie(Should have been the other way round, but then, the one which comes first usually forms the greater impression). I'm glad the scripwriters did not destroy the essence of the story, which is (though simple), a charming tale!<br /><br />Anyway, coming back to the story, it is about four adventurous children, who accidentally discover the magical world of Narnia behind a wardrobe in an old strange house. But all in not well in this magical land! It's under the reign of the evil queen -- the WHite Witch! Together with the help of a Magnificant Lion -- Aslan, the children go on to kill the witch and themselves become the Four Kings(and Queens!) of Narnia.<br /><br />Though lacking much plot twists, I loved it simply because it was so easy-flowing! I altually wished I had a naughty kid near me whom I could catch hold of and read the book aloud to! I would love to see his wide-eyed amazement when Lucy would open the wardrobe for the first time, see his dissapointment when Edmund would betray them, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[hear his sigh when Aslan would sacrifice his life, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and catch that gleeful smile when they would win the Battle!<br /><br />Maybe someday, sometime!<br /><br />P.S: I really liked the dedication. <br /><br />It read:<br /><br /><em> " My dear Lucy,<br />I wrote this story for you, but when I began it I had not realized that girls grow quicker than books. As a result you are already too old for fairy tales, and by the time it is printed and bound you will be older still. But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again. You can then take it down from some upper shelf, dust it, and tell me what you think of it. I shall probably be too deaf to hear, and too old to understand, a word you say, but I shall still be,<br />your affectionate Godfather,<br /><a title="C.S.Lewis" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=C.S.Lewis">C.S.Lewis</a>."</em><br /><br /><strong>
  <em> Dear God, I hope I'm NEVER too old for fairy tales! </em>
</strong><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1433814296">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1433814296" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8759700-denisse" class="left imgcol" title="Denisse"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451853606p2/8759700.jpg" alt="Denisse" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1433814296?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8759700-denisse" class="user" title="Denisse">Denisse</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1433814296">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8363330132017910640">A beautiful read. There's no other way to say it. The story knows what it is, knows the public it wants to catch, children, but leaves details every now and then for we the grown ups to appreciate, turning a great children story into <strong>one of The fantasy novels this world has.</strong> Great messages, and unique style. Light but direct to the point, like all children stories should be. <strong>The lion, the witch and the wardrobe is a must for all the lovers of the genre. </strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="184" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451854523ra/17613698.gif" /><br /><br /><br />Solo hay una cosa que de verdad se puede d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8363330132017910640">A beautiful read. There's no other way to say it. The story knows what it is, knows the public it wants to catch, children, but leaves details every now and then for we the grown ups to appreciate, turning a great children story into <strong>one of The fantasy novels this world has.</strong> Great messages, and unique style. Light but direct to the point, like all children stories should be. <strong>The lion, the witch and the wardrobe is a must for all the lovers of the genre. </strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="184" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451854523ra/17613698.gif" /><br /><br /><br />Solo hay una cosa que de verdad se puede decir sobre los cuentos para niños: <strong>Si es una buena historia, se puede disfrutar a cualquier edad.</strong><br /><br />Llena de enseñanzas perfectas para los pequeños y con detalles muy interesantes para los grandes, Narnia en definitiva es una gran historia. No es ningun secreto las millones de referencias biblicas que tiene, aunque estoy segura que no capte todas. Pero todo <strong>Aslan</strong> es una hermosa referencia que adore por completo, sobretodo porque es un leon y Narnia una tierra de bestias parlantes, asi como tambien todo lo relacionado con Edmund.<br /><br />Lo que mas me emociono fue darme cuenta de la <strong>increible adaptacion</strong> que tiene. Increible nivel: Catching Fire. O sea, las ligas mayores. Respetaron muchisimos detalles, gracias Disney! <br /><br />La batalla entre el bien y el mal puede escucharse muy <em>mainstream</em> pero cuando se escribe bien, se nota. <br /><br />Este libro no esta hecho para la gente que solo disfruta de las descripciones y explicaciones largas y a detalle. Es un clasico de niños y te encontraras con muchos <em>"pero eso no es importante para esta historia, asi que volvamos a..."</em> o varios <em>"porque asi son las cosas"</em>. Si estas en el mood para algo asi, El leon, la bruja y el armario cumplira con la funcion a la perfeccion. Altamente recomendado. Seguire leyendo las cronicas a como se fueron publicando. <br /><br />Gran forma de terminar el año. Sorry por tener la reseña hasta el 2016 xDlol</span>

          <a href="/book/show/45624.O_Le_o_a_Feiticeira_e_o_Guarda_Roupa" title="Capa Mole 9789722330206" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1151773011">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11214731947878053520">A primeira leitura que fiz em 2015 foi o "O Sobrinho do Mágico" porque tinha como objetivo para este ano ler todas as Crónicas de Nárnia por ordem cronológica, mas tal não se concretizou. Nem a ordem, nem a leitura em si. Acabei por deixar passar os meses e nunca mais continuei a minha aventura, até que chegou o mês de Dezembro!<br /><br />Achei que não haveria mês melhor para ler "O Leão, A Feiticeira e o Guarda-Roupa" por isso peguei nele. O filme é um mais especiais para mim porque vi e revi e revi e o e</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11214731947878053520">A primeira leitura que fiz em 2015 foi o "O Sobrinho do Mágico" porque tinha como objetivo para este ano ler todas as Crónicas de Nárnia por ordem cronológica, mas tal não se concretizou. Nem a ordem, nem a leitura em si. Acabei por deixar passar os meses e nunca mais continuei a minha aventura, até que chegou o mês de Dezembro!<br /><br />Achei que não haveria mês melhor para ler "O Leão, A Feiticeira e o Guarda-Roupa" por isso peguei nele. O filme é um mais especiais para mim porque vi e revi e revi e o encanto nunca se perdeu e admito que estava com medo que isso acontecesse ao ler a história original. Mas não. Achei o filme muito fiel à obra de C.S.Lewis e, embora ainda ocupe um lugar mais especial no meu coração, gostei imenso desta leitura. Penso que o que também contribuiu muito para isso foi a minha edição que tem ilustrações muito bonitas a cores de Pauline Bayne e mesmo a disposição das páginas é muito prazerosa de ler. Mas claro que a história em si é o que mais importa e custa-me imenso a entender como toda ela nos é contada de uma forma tão fácil de visualizar mas em tão poucas páginas! O autor tem uma escrita magnífica e incrivelmente simples que nunca se torna aborrecida. Há sempre algum conflito, algo a acontecer. Ah e o inglês não é complicado ;) <br /><br />Aconselho este livrinho a toda a gente, especialmente nesta época! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11214731947878053520">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=re-read" class="actionLinkLite">re-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=children-s-books" class="actionLinkLite">children-s-books</a>,

              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25953366">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17415612686661697323">Every time I read this wonderful story, it's like catching up with an old friend. I've read this particular Narnia book so many different times, but it never ever gets old. I love the fact that I can pick up this (or any of the other Narnia books, for that matter) and step into a whole other world. I also particularly enjoyed the fact that I found that I could relate with each one of the children, although I must say that I was partial to Lucy! Additionally, I love the role Aslan plays in this b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17415612686661697323">Every time I read this wonderful story, it's like catching up with an old friend. I've read this particular Narnia book so many different times, but it never ever gets old. I love the fact that I can pick up this (or any of the other Narnia books, for that matter) and step into a whole other world. I also particularly enjoyed the fact that I found that I could relate with each one of the children, although I must say that I was partial to Lucy! Additionally, I love the role Aslan plays in this book because I think the ultimate sacrifice that he chooses to make shows how innately good he is. Even though this book is an easy read for me now at 18, this is one of those kinds of stories that you can never grow too old for. I really liked the line C.S. Lewis wrote in his dedication: "But some day you will be old enough to start reading fairy tales again." Isn't that the truth? Overall, I'd definitely have to call this a classic that everyone should read at some point or another. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer238957877203893031">I am tempted to give this book a zero but the idea of going through the wardrobe to another land is fantastic. Everything else, however, is not fantastic, including:<br /><br />The over-the-top Christian allegory.<br />The complete absence of dramatic tension - the characters are static and the conclusion is foregone.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText238957877203893031">I am tempted to give this book a zero but the idea of going through the wardrobe to another land is fantastic. Everything else, however, is not fantastic, including:<br /><br />The over-the-top Christian allegory.<br />The complete absence of dramatic tension - the characters are static and the conclusion is foregone. There is nothing to keep you reading, to challenge you, or to even vaguely interest you.<br />The writing is mediocre at best.<br />The dialogue is mediocre at best.<br /><br />Awful book, it as if someone read Matthew through John, and then said these four gospels are good but it would take a master writer to retell them with talking animals and have it be worse to the point of complete boredom.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_15886491">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15886491" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/904335-abi" class="left imgcol" title="Abi"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1224713972p2/904335.jpg" alt="Abi" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/15886491?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 20, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/904335-abi" class="user" title="Abi">Abi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1551102.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780006716631" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Smug Christians</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=children-s-literature" class="actionLinkLite">children-s-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=20th-century" class="actionLinkLite">20th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/904335-abi?shelf=ireland" class="actionLinkLite">ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15886491">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10368287303852919266">I read this when I was little (I would guess about 7, 8 or 9), and I didn't pick up on all the Christianity references, despite them being SO overt. What I did feel was astonishment that the children all loved Aslan so much, when I thought he was massively sanctimonious and sickening as a character. I could not <em>stand</em> that lion. I didn't want the Snow Queen to win exactly, I didn't like her either... but at the same time I wanted someone to show the lion up, or force him to do something INTERESTI</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10368287303852919266">I read this when I was little (I would guess about 7, 8 or 9), and I didn't pick up on all the Christianity references, despite them being SO overt. What I did feel was astonishment that the children all loved Aslan so much, when I thought he was massively sanctimonious and sickening as a character. I could not <em>stand</em> that lion. I didn't want the Snow Queen to win exactly, I didn't like her either... but at the same time I wanted someone to show the lion up, or force him to do something INTERESTING that would show he wasn't an unmitigating goody-goody. Aslan was just... ugh, completely two dimensional in his perfection, and so smug and arrogant. He was <em>unbearable</em>. I enjoyed the fantasy, but the element that I now realise (and am astonished that I ever didn't realise) is the Christian allegory made me scornful of the character who was supposed to be the supreme 'goody' and left a bitter taste in my mouth that ultimately ruined it. I enjoyed other books in the series more, some more than others (enjoyment increased exponentially as Aslan's involvement decreased), but this one was the worst.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10368287303852919266">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_18368388">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18368388" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="left imgcol" title="Werner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" alt="Werner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18368388?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="user" title="Werner">Werner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone (of any age) who loves fantasy, and/or serious Christian literature</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18368388">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12911159733738703790">While I first read this book in the mid-70s, I read it again to my wife a couple of years ago (we both loved it then as much as I did the first time). Most people know that C. S. Lewis was an effective Christian nonfiction apologist, using the tools of reason and logic to build the philosophical case for Christian faith. But he ultimately became convinced that an even more effective apologetic is available through the "truth of art," the instinctive and emotional appeal that stories exert -- esp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12911159733738703790">While I first read this book in the mid-70s, I read it again to my wife a couple of years ago (we both loved it then as much as I did the first time). Most people know that C. S. Lewis was an effective Christian nonfiction apologist, using the tools of reason and logic to build the philosophical case for Christian faith. But he ultimately became convinced that an even more effective apologetic is available through the "truth of art," the instinctive and emotional appeal that stories exert -- especially the kinds of stories that draw on the deep, mythical archetypes of fantasy to illuminate the real universe. The Chronicles of Narnia, his classic fantasy series, was the fruit of that discovery, set in Narnia, a magical land whose world lies in another universe, in which magic works and time moves differently than it does here, and in which Christ is incarnate as the great talking lion Aslan. This first book of the series presents one of the most powerful symbolic literary presentations of the Christian gospel ever written. Although the intended audience, in Lewis' mind, was children (and his author's various direct addresses to the readers presuppose this), there is nothing invidiously "juvenile" about the quality of the writing; it can be enthusiastically appreciated by anyone who loves tales of imagination and adventure, fantasy and wonder; and the truths here, like those in Jesus' parables, are simple enough to speak to children but profound enough to challenge adults.<br /><br />This is the "first" book of the series in the sense of first to be written (and usually the first to be read). However, The Magicians Nephew is a prequel which describes the creation of Narnia, and the origins of the White Witch and of the wardrobe that serves as a gateway to Narnia; Lewis himself recommended that this prequel be read first.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12911159733738703790">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4361664385314090991">If you like the Chronicles of Narnia, you should check out the "King of the Trees" series by William Burt. (His Goodreads page is: <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/319026.William_D_Burt" target="_blank">http://www.goodread</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4361664385314090991">If you like the Chronicles of Narnia, you should check out the "King of the Trees" series by William Burt. (His Goodreads page is: <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/319026.William_D_Burt" target="_blank">http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/...</a>) I've read all six books in his series. I hear the seventh one is coming out this summer. I think they are better than Narnia, but that's just my opinion. They make nice readalouds, because each book has a pronunciation guide and glossary at the back. The series web site is <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.greencloaks.com" target="_blank">http://www.greencloaks.com</a>. It's a fun site.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1415117494">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1415117494" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/47862327-issac" class="left imgcol" title="Issac"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1444697866p2/47862327.jpg" alt="Issac" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1415117494?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/47862327-issac" class="user" title="Issac">Issac</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/100915.The_Lion_the_Witch_and_the_Wardrobe" title="Paperback 9780060764890" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer1415117494'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1415117494">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10918096822177992998">My greatest disappointment in 'The Screwtape Letters' was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The closest he got to defining goodness was that you could tell the good people from the vague aura of light that surrounded them--and which even shone in their cat. In this book, the cat is much bigger.<br /><br />Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10918096822177992998">My greatest disappointment in 'The Screwtape Letters' was that Lewis was not able to demonstrate what made his good people good or his bad people bad. The closest he got to defining goodness was that you could tell the good people from the vague aura of light that surrounded them--and which even shone in their cat. In this book, the cat is much bigger.<br /><br />Aslan had no character, he was just a big, dull stand-in. Lewis often tells us how great he is, but never demonstrates what it is that makes him great or impressive. Sure, he helps the kids, but all that makes him is a plot facilitator. He also has his big Jesus moment, but that has the same problem as the original: if he already knows that there will be no lasting negative outcome, how much of a sacrifice is it, really?<br /><br />But then, Aslan isn't based on the original fig-cursing, church-rejecting, rebel Jesus, but the whitewashed version. Like Mickey Mouse, Jesus started out as an oddball troublemaker with his fair share of personality, but becoming the smiling face of a multinational organization bent on world domination takes a lot out of a mascot, whether your magic castle is in California or Rome.<br /><br />Such a visible figure must become universally appealing, universally friendly and loving, lest some subset of followers feel left out. And it's this 'Buddy Christ' tradition from which Aslan springs. Devoid of insight, wisdom, or charm, Aslan is just here to do all the things that our protagonists can't do.<br /><br />This also beggars the question: why didn't Aslan just take care of all this stuff long before the kids arrived? Why did all the animals and fairies and giants have to suffer the pain of an endless winter? We're never given any good reason Aslan had to wait for the kids--since in the end, he does it all on his own, anyways. Sure, Lewis mentions something vague about a prophecy, but in fantasy, prophecy is always a bandaid authors stick over their plot holes: 'Uh, the shlubby nobody is a hero because the prophecy says he is--he defeats the ultimate evil because the prophecy says he can'.<br /><br />The only thing the kids do is help run the battle, but this is only necessary because Aslan is absent, and he's only absent because the kids screwed up, meaning the entire thing would have gone off without a hitch if they had never showed up in the first place.<br /><br />In that regard, I have to say Lewis did an excellent job boiling down Christianity into a fable, and leaving the problem of evil completely intact. Some readers suggest that Aslan lets the queen take over to teach the kids a lesson, but is it really worthwhile to let all the inhabitants of a kingdom suffer a century of misery just to teach a few kids about the true meaning of friendship?<br /><br />The villain is just as poorly-constructed, and seems less concerned with defeating her enemies than with being pointlessly capricious. She manages to trick one of the children, but instead of taking advantage of this fact, she immediately makes it clear that she tricked him. I mean, how did someone that incompetent take over in the first place?<br /><br />Selectively stupid characters are silly and convenient, especially as villains, because this completely undermines their role as foil. It is impressive when characters overcome challenges, but not when challenges simply crumble before them. The children are lucky the Queen was more of a fart-stealing Old Nick than a Miltonian Satan, otherwise they never would have stood a chance.<br /><br />It is interesting to look at how many Christian authors have tried to reconcile their faith with complex fairy mythologies; not that Christianity doesn't have its own magical fairy tales, but these other traditions are not exactly compatible. Dante has Virgil lead him through hell, the Buddha was made into a saint, holidays were given new meanings (even if they often kept old symbols and names), and magical monsters were also given a place in the new faith.<br /><br />In the Middle Ages, monks compiled 'Bestiaries', which described the roles of dragons, unicorns, and real animals in Christian synbolism; there were even century-spanning debates about whether dog-headed men were descended from Adam. These books were rarely accurate, but allowed Christian theology to adopt many stories and superstitions from earlier periods; for instance, the connection between unicorns and virginity or the belief that pelicans fed their own blood to their young, in imitation of communion.<br /><br />So Lewis' attempt to take myth and adapt it to a Christian cosmology is hardly new--there is a long and storied tradition explored throughout the Chivalric period and recognizable today in books like The Once and Future King, but Lewis doesn't do a very good job of reconciling these disparate mythologies.<br /><br />Like most Protestants, Lewis' religion was a modern one--not magical and mystical, but reasonable and utilitarian. He did not draw on the elaborate, convoluted apocrypha of hallucinatory monsters and miracles that mystics obsess over, instead, he made a small, sane, reasonable magical world--which rather defeats the point. It is unfortunate that many of today's readers think of Lewis' writings as defining English fairy tales, since his late additions to the genre are not original, nor are they particularly well-executed examples.<br /><br />Many authors have come to the genre with much more imagination, a deeper sense of wonder, and a more far-reaching exploration of magic. We have examples from Kipling, Lewis Carroll, Dunsany, Eddison, Morris, and even modern updates by Gaiman and Clarke. Lewis, like Tolkien, may be a well-known example, but both are rather short-sighted, and neither one achieves as much as the many talented authors who came before.<br /><br />I'm not saying Lewis is bad, merely that he is unremarkable, and is hardly preeminent in fantasy, or even in children's fantasy. However, I do think his fundamental message is a bad one, even if he didn't realize he was creating it.<br /><br />In all his worlds, all his stories, he takes the sorts of people he dislikes, defines them as 'evil', then sets himself apart from them. There is no attempt to comprehend or to come to mutual understanding. I cannot respect a book which encourages people to vilify what they don't understand and to call isolation righteous. If any worldview deserves the epithet of 'evil', it is the sort of willful, prideful, self-indulgent ignorance Lewis displays.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10918096822177992998">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
